        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.4c14093"><meta property="og:title" content="Sodium-Ion Storage in Na4VMn(PO4)3/C Porous Nanorods Electrode"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c14093/asset/images/acsami.4c14093.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Na4VMn(PO4)3 (NVMP) is a prospective cathode for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) due to its low cost and extraordinary structural stability. However, poor conductivity restricts the electrochemical performance of NVMP, which limits its development and application. Herein, carbon-encapsulated NVMP porous nanorods are prepared via a convenient electrospinning method. The prepared one-dimensional (1D) structure can effectively accelerate the transmission rate of electrons, and the porous structure of NVMP/C nanorods can increase the contact area between the electrolyte and electrode material, which can enhance the diffusion rate of sodium ions. Owing to this advantageous nanoarchitecture, the obtained NVMP/C porous nanorods delivered an initial reversible capacity of 90.9 mA h·g–1 at 0.2 C and a discharge capacity of 68.0 mA h g–1 at 5.0 C. NVMP/C can also achieve a good cycling stability with ideal capacity retentions of 90.4% and 89.8% after 800 cycles at 1 and 3 C, respectively. Moreover, even at a temperature of −30 °C at 0.3 C, the discharge capability was up to 40.1% of that at 20 °C, suggesting an impressive low-temperature tolerance. In situ X-ray diffraction demonstrated that there was no crystal distortion of the NVMP/C electrode during charge and discharge. This work can provide a high-performance strategy on modification of polyanion-positive electrode materials for SIBs."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c14093/asset/images/acsami.4c14093.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="9318856cdf7b54b8-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="9318856cdf7b54b8-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '2958b8c1-3e25-49e1-9239-0bba8dae8157'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="9318856cdf7b54b8-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

<script nonce="9318856cdf7b54b8-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Sodium-Ion Storage in Na&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;VMn(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;/C Porous Nanorods Electrode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c14093", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Sodium-Ion Storage in Na&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;VMn(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;/C Porous Nanorods Electrode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c14093", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Sodium-Ion Storage in Na&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;VMn(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;/C Porous Nanorods Electrode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c14093", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Sodium-Ion Storage in Na&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;VMn(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;/C Porous Nanorods Electrode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c14093", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Sodium-Ion Storage in Na&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;VMn(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;/C Porous Nanorods Electrode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c14093", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Sodium-Ion Storage in Na&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;VMn(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;/C Porous Nanorods Electrode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c14093", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Sodium-Ion Storage in Na&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;VMn(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;/C Porous Nanorods Electrode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c14093", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Sodium-Ion Storage in Na&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;VMn(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;/C Porous Nanorods Electrode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c14093", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Sodium-Ion Storage in Na&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;VMn(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;/C Porous Nanorods Electrode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c14093", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

class="articleMetrics_count"><h2>Citations</h2><div class="articleMetrics-val"><a href="#citeThis" class="internalNav">1</a></div></div></div></div><div class="articleMetrics_about"><div class="base dropBlock"><a href="#" data-db-target-for="aboutArticleMetrics" aria-describedby="about-article-metrics" class="article_aboutLink">Learn about these metrics</a><div data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span class="sr-only">close</span></button><p>Article Views are the COUNTER-compliant sum of full text article downloads since November 2008 (both PDF and HTML) across all institutions and individuals. These metrics are regularly updated to reflect usage leading up to the last few days.</p><p>Citations are the number of other articles citing this article, calculated by Crossref and updated daily.<a href="https://www.crossref.org/services/cited-by/"> Find more information about Crossref citation counts.</a></p><p>The Altmetric Attention Score is a quantitative measure of the attention that a research article has received online. Clicking on the donut icon will load a page at altmetric.com with additional details about the score and the social media presence for the given article. Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c14093/asset/images/medium/am4c14093_0007.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c14093/asset/images/large/am4c14093_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c14093&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Na<sub>4</sub>VMn(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVMP) is a prospective cathode for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) due to its low cost and extraordinary structural stability. However, poor conductivity restricts the electrochemical performance of NVMP, which limits its development and application. Herein, carbon-encapsulated NVMP porous nanorods are prepared via a convenient electrospinning method. The prepared one-dimensional (1D) structure can effectively accelerate the transmission rate of electrons, and the porous structure of NVMP/C nanorods can increase the contact area between the electrolyte and electrode material, which can enhance the diffusion rate of sodium ions. Owing to this advantageous nanoarchitecture, the obtained NVMP/C porous nanorods delivered an initial reversible capacity of 90.9 mA h·g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2 C and a discharge capacity of 68.0 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 5.0 C. NVMP/C can also achieve a good cycling stability with ideal capacity retentions of 90.4% and 89.8% after 800 cycles at 1 and 3 C, respectively. Moreover, even at a temperature of −30 °C at 0.3 C, the discharge capability was up to 40.1% of that at 20 °C, suggesting an impressive low-temperature tolerance. In situ X-ray diffraction demonstrated that there was no crystal distortion of the NVMP/C electrode during charge and discharge. This work can provide a high-performance strategy on modification of polyanion-positive electrode materials for SIBs.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D69221%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DHongbo%2BWang%252C%2BJiayi%2BZhang%252C%2BJingjing%2BSun%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D16%26issueNum%3D50%26contentID%3Dacsami.4c14093%26title%3DSodium-Ion%2BStorage%2Bin%2BNa4VMn%2528PO4%25293%252FC%2BPorous%2BNanorods%2BElectrode%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D69228%26publicationDate%3DDecember%2B2024" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2024 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">

                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Na4vmn3&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Na4VMn%28PO4%293">Na<sub>4</sub>VMn(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub></a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Electrospinning&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_electrospinning">electrospinning</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Porous+Structure&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_porous%20structure">porous structure</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Nanocomposite&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_nanocomposite">nanocomposite</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are viewed as a hopeful choice in grid-side energy storage, and electrode materials are the most vital components of SIBs, which determine the electrochemical performances of SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 1 to 5">(1−5)</a> Usually, a cathode material plays an important role in electrode materials, and researchers have been committed to developing novel high-performance cathodes within these years. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 6 to 12">(6−12)</a> Among them, polyanion-positive electrode materials have attracted widespread attention because of their good cycling stability. Here, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, as a NASICON-structured cathode material, has gained extensive study. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 13 to 18">(13−18)</a> To further reduce the toxicity, half of the aforementioned V atoms are substituted by Mn, and Na<sub>4</sub>VMn(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVMP) has been reported, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> which is considered as a candidate for the next generation of polyanion cathodes in SIBs. However, the limited inherent electronic conductivity in polyanionic compounds constrains the attainment of outstanding electrochemical performance.</div><div class="NLM_p">To overcome this challenge, researchers have adopted multiple approaches, such as carbon coating, constructing heterogeneous composites, and particle size nanoscale. The former is a valid means to enhance its electronic conductivity. For example, NVMP/C can significantly improve its electrochemical performances after carbon coating. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 20 to 23">(20−23)</a> Constructing a heterogeneous composite has also been engineering applied in the modification of the polyanion cathode material, which can improve the structural stability of the material. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25 ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24 ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 24 to 26">(24−26)</a> In addition, the nanoparticle-sized cathode material can effectively decrease the diffusion pathways of ions, enhancing the ionic conductivity of the cathode material to obtain excellent electrochemical performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28 ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27 ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 27 to 29">(27−29)</a> However, these improvement strategies also have certain limitations. Among them, carbon coating increases the transport resistance of ions and reduces the diffusion rate of ions. Constructing heterogeneous structures faces issues such as a complex preparation process and material compatibility requirement. Particle size nanoscale tends to lead to particle agglomeration and needs to design a rational architecture. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 30 and 31">(30,31)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">As is well known, one of the beneficial tactics for boosting the electrochemical properties is to design one-dimensional (1D) nanoporous structure, which not only provides a short transport path but also has a facile strain relaxation related to the structural transformation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 32 and 33">(32,33)</a> Meanwhile, electrospinning is a proven method for building nanostructures characterized by superior ion and electron bifunctional transport properties. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 34 to 37">(34−37)</a> In this paper, carbon-coated NVMP porous nanorods were thus synthesized through an electrospinning technology using polyvinylpyrrolidone (PVP) as the carbon source, which not only meets the nanoengineering of NVMP but also realizes the carbon encapsulation as well as the architecture. The synthesized NVMP/C exhibits a capacity output approaching theoretical capacity delivery and a long cycle lifespan.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Materials and Methods</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Materials Synthesis</h3><div class="NLM_p last">By vigorous stirring, 2 g of polyvinylpyrrolidone with a molecular weight of 1,300,000 was dissolved in 8 mL of deionized water at 60 °C to form solution A. At the same time, 8 mmol of citric acid, 4 mmol of NH<sub>4</sub>VO<sub>3</sub>, 4 mmol of Mn(CH<sub>3</sub>COO)<sub>2</sub>·4H<sub>2</sub>O, 12 mmol of NH<sub>4</sub>H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>, and 16 mmol of CH<sub>3</sub>COONa·3H<sub>2</sub>O were dissolved into 8 mL of deionized water with vigorous stirring at 60 °C to form a uniform green-colored solution B. Thereafter, solution B was dropwise added into solution A, and the spinning solution was obtained after stirring the mixture for 2 h at room temperature. Subsequently, a plastic syringe fitted with a 22-gauge metallic needle was filled with the spinning solution, and a voltage of 25 kV was applied to the needle, with respect to a rotating collector that was wrapped in Al foil. A 10 cm gap was maintained with an ejection velocity of 0.8 mL·h<sup>–1</sup>. Upon completion of electrospinning, the precursor was calcined in a nitrogen-filled tube furnace, first for 2 h at 350 °C and then for 8 h at 700 °C, and finally ground by a human operator with a mortar and pestle to obtain NVMP/C porous nanorods. According to a previous report, NVMP was also synthesized by a sol–gel procedure. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a></div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Material Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The powder X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns of the materials were carried out by a Bruker D8 Advance (Cu Kα radiation wavelength: 1.5418 Å). Raman spectrum was recorded through a Horiba LabRAM HR (laser wavelength: 532 nm). The carbon content was analyzed by a Hitachi STA200 in air (temperature ramping rate: 10 °C min<sup>–1</sup>) and an element CS instrument (Leco CS844). The morphology was characterized by field-emission scanning electron microscopy (SEM; Zeiss GeminiSEM300) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM; JEOL JEM2100F) equipped with an energy-dispersive spectrometer (EDS; Oxford IE250). Micromeritics ASAP2460 was applied to test the specific surface area and porosity data. In situ XRD was conducted on a Malvern Panalytical Empyrean XRD.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The working electrode is made by coating an Al foil with a slurry composed of the active material (90 wt %), super <i>P</i> conductive agent (5 wt %), and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF, 5 wt %) in an <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP) solvent. The cathodes were punched into disks of 12 mm after drying, and the loading mass of active materials was ca. 4.40 mg·cm<sup>–2</sup>. Whatman glass fiber was applied as the separator, and the electrolyte was 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> dissolved in ethylene carbonate (EC), diethyl carbonate (DEC), and propylene carbonate (PC) solution (2:3:5 in volume) with 5 vol % FEC. An amount of 80 μL of electrolyte was added into each assembled coin cells by using a Mettler pipet-lite. In the CR2025 full cells, hard carbon (HC, bought from Japan Kureha Battery Material Co, Ltd.) was used as the anode, and the preparation of the negative electrode was the same as that of the cathode, which had a single-side coating and a diameter of 12 mm unless the mass loading of ca. 1.72 mg·cm<sup>–2</sup>. Prior to assembly of the full cells, HC electrodes were presodiated in Na metal coin cells within 0–2.5 V for three cycles. Then, HC electrodes were disassembled at charge 2.5 V and used as negative electrodes in sodium-ion full cells, in which the mass ratio of HC to NVMP/C is about 1:2.56. The galvanostatic charge–discharge measurements were examined by a LANB test system (BT2018R, Wuhan). The low- and high-temperature performances were investigated by using a high/low-temperature tank (Suzhou Chenguang Testing Equipment Co. Ltd.). Cyclic voltammetry (CV) tests were performed by an CHI700E electrochemical workstation (Shanghai Chenhua).</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a> displays the synthesis procedures of the NVMP/C porous nanorods. To begin, the spinning solution was electrospun to form a flexible membrane precursor (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c14093/suppl_file/am4c14093_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>), and then, the fibrous membrane was calcined in N<sub>2</sub>. The carbonized material has a fragile property (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c14093/suppl_file/am4c14093_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>) and was then ground into powder for further characterization and measurement. The Rietveld refinement analysis was performed, and the diffraction pattern of NMVP/C is ascribed to the trigonal phase with a space group of <i>R</i><span class="overline">3</span><i>c</i> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a). It is revealed that a typical NASICON structure, which has large ion transport channels.The cell parameters and volume are calculated to be <i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 8.9567 Å, <i>c</i> = 21.5580 Å, and <i>V</i> = 1497.74 Å<sup>3</sup> (reliability factor: <i>R</i><sub>p</sub> = 4.62% and <i>R</i><sub>wp</sub> = 6.00%), respectively, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c14093/suppl_file/am4c14093_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>, agreeing with the previously published findings. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref40 ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39 ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 39 to 41">(39−41)</a> The peaks marked at 1369 and 1597 cm<sup>–1</sup> were indexed to the disorder-induced phonon mode (D band) and the graphite band (G band) of carbonaceous materials. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> the relative intensity ratio related to the D and G bands is estimated to be 1.34, indicating the existence of amorphous carbon with a low crystallinity. The amount of carbon was first estimated by the TGA curve. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c, the first drop of weight (about 1.1 wt %) is the dehydrating of adsorbed water (below 200 °C). While the temperature reached about 300 °C, the second weight loss began and slowed down as the temperature rose to 500 °C, during which the weight loss (about 19.3 wt %) is mainly caused by the combustion of carbon with O<sub>2</sub> in air. To further determine the precise carbon content, NVMP/C was measured in an elemental CS analyzer. From the carbon analysis (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c14093/suppl_file/am4c14093_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>), the percentage of carbon is 17.51 wt %, agreeing with the TGA result.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c14093/asset/images/medium/am4c14093_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c14093/asset/images/large/am4c14093_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Schematic diagrams for the synthesis of NVMP/C porous nanorods.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c14093/asset/images/large/am4c14093_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c14093&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c14093/asset/images/medium/am4c14093_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c14093/asset/images/large/am4c14093_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Structural characterization of the as-synthesized NVMP/C. (a) XRD Rietveld refinement pattern, (b) Raman spectrum, and (c) TGA curve.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c14093/asset/images/large/am4c14093_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c14093&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a,b displays the SEM images of the membrane precursor, which consists of cross-connected fibers interlinking into a 3D network structure. After high-temperature calcination, the surface is rough and presents voids, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c–d. Additionally, the diameter of the carbonized fibers is hundreds of nanometers, and some of them closely aggregate together to form a larger one. When vigorously ground and broken the length, the final obtained NVMP/C powder mainly consists of nanorods according to the TEM image in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>f demonstrates the TEM image of a single nanorod. The NVMP/C nanorod consists of interconnected nanoparticles, which are embedded in the carbon matrix. Such 1D structure is not only beneficial for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion along the radial direction but also the continuous carbon matrix can provide convenient electron transport along the axial direction. The HR-TEM image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>g) further reveals NVMP nanocrystals with an amorphous carbon, and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>h displays the relevant inverse fast Fourier transform (FFT) electron diffraction pattern, in which the space of 0.467 nm matches well with the (104) plane of trigonal NVMP/C. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>i, the high-angle annular dark-field (HAADF) STEM image displays the bright-dark contrast of a single NVMP/C nanorod, and the relevant EDS mappings suggest that the elements of Na, V, Mn, P, O, and C are uniformly distributed. In <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c14093/suppl_file/am4c14093_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>, the isotherms present a slight hysteresis at a relatively high pressure, suggesting a mesoporous characteristic of the material. From the pore size distribution curve, it can be seen that the pore size is mainly less than 10 nm. Based on the BET testing, NVMP/C nanorods have a special surface area of only 2.70 m<sup>2</sup>·g<sup>–1</sup>. Therefore, it is concluded that NVMP/C consists of nanorods with massive surface pores. Furthermore, the micrographs of NVMP synthesized by the sol–gel method are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c14093/suppl_file/am4c14093_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>, which reveals a different morphology with large average particle size.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c14093/asset/images/medium/am4c14093_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c14093/asset/images/large/am4c14093_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Morphological and microstructural characterizations of the membrane precursor and as-synthesized NVMP/C. (a–d) SEM images, (e–h) TEM images and HR-TEM image, and (i) HAADF-STEM image and corresponding EDS mappings of a single NVMP/C nanorod.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c14093/asset/images/large/am4c14093_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c14093&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The measurement of Na storage performance was first evaluated in 2.5–3.8 V. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a exhibits the CV profiles for the NMVP/C electrode, and two couples of main redox peaks at 3.47/3.35 V and 3.68/3.51 V were found, ascribing to the V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>3+</sup> and Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>2+</sup> redox couples, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 43 and 44">(43,44)</a> Note that significant reversibility was attained, exhibiting unnoticeable discrepancies apart from a minor reduction peak at around 3.15 V, which gradually disappeared with cycling and is a common phenomenon in the NVMP materials and other cathodes with a NASICON structure. It was the result of the rearrangement or change of the local environment for Na<sup>+</sup> during the reduction of vanadium ions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45 ref46 ref47 ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45 ref46 ref47 ref48" aria-label="reference 45 to 48">(45−48)</a> The charge–discharge profiles exhibit two distinct voltage platforms at 0.2 C in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b, indicating a two stepwise Na<sup>+</sup> extraction–insertion process. The initial discharge capacity is 90.9 mA h·g<sup>–1</sup>, which approaches the value calculated with the theoretical specific capacity of NVMP multiplied by its mass fraction in the active material. After 100 cycles, the NVMP/C electrode presents an ideal capacity retention of 95.1% (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c), and the Coulombic efficiency is close to 100%, indicating its superior cyclability and reversibility. For comparative purpose, the rate performances of NVMP and NVMP/C were examined in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d. As the current rates increase, the capacity differences between NVMP and NVMP/C become much clearer. Especially, NVMP/C exhibits a superior capacity at higher rates, which demonstrates a better high-rate performance for NVMP/C. The discharge capacities of NVMP/C at 0.5, 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, and 10 C are 88.7, 85.9, 82.4, 78.8, 74.4, 68.0, and 29.2 mA h·g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Upon completion of 40 cycles, the initial capacity can be recovered to 99.6% (90.5 mA h·g<sup>–1</sup>) as the rate is reverted back to 0.2 C. Moreover, long-term cycling stability of NVMP/C was specially examined (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>e). For the capacities of 0.5, 1, 3, and 5 C after 700, 800, 800, and 1000 cycles, the capacity retentions are 91.1%, 90.4%, 89.8%, and 84.0%, respectively. It is noted that the electrode progressively approaches a stable state after 200 cycles at 5 C, and over the last 800 cycles, the performance exhibits highly impressive cycling stability. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c14093/suppl_file/am4c14093_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S3</a> further compared the electrochemical performances with those of some other reported cathodes. It can be seen that the NVMP/C electrode with nanorods delivers desirable long-term cyclability. Besides, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>f also describes the charge–discharge profiles under various temperature conditions from 20 to −30 °C at 0.3 C. As the testing temperature decreases, it is found that MVMP/C presents an additional voltage platform of ca. 2.9 V during discharge, attributing to the sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation kinetic within the NVMP cathode at low temperature. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> Compared with the discharge capability under the temperature of 20 °C, the NVMP/C electrode could attain 97.8, 94.3, 88.1, 73.3, and 40.1% of the initial capacity when tested at 10, 0, −10, −20, and −30 °C, respectively, indicating an impressive low-temperature tolerance. Unfortunately, MVMP/C exhibited a terrible high-temperature cycling performance when tested at 60 °C as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c14093/suppl_file/am4c14093_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>. which is also a common phenomenon in the Mn-based materials mainly due to the quickly dissolved manganese ions related to the Jahn–Teller effect at elevated temperature. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50 ref51 ref52" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref50 ref51 ref52" aria-label="reference 50 to 52">(50−52)</a></div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c14093/asset/images/medium/am4c14093_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c14093/asset/images/large/am4c14093_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Electrochemical measurements of NVMP/C in half cells. (a) CV profiles, (b) galvanostatic charge–discharge curves, (c) cycling performances, (d) rate performance at various current, (e) long-term cycling at different rates, and (f) charge–discharge curves at 0.3 C in temperature range from 20 to −30 °C, 1 C = 110 mA·g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c14093/asset/images/large/am4c14093_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c14093&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a shows the CV profiles measured (2.5–3.9 V). As the scan rate increased, it can be seen that both the currents matching with the oxidation/reduction peaks exhibit significant enhancements, while the overall configuration of the CV plots is well preserved, hinting a desirable kinetics for sodium ions extraction and insertion during desodiation and sodiation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> According to the Randles–Sevcik formula, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a> namely, <i>i</i><sub>p</sub> = 2.69 × 10<sup>5</sup><i>n</i><sup>3/2</sup>A<i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+1/2</sup>C<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>v<sup>1/2</sup>, Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients (<i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>) were computed. From the <i>i</i><sub>p</sub> versus v<sup>1/2</sup> plots at the peaks (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b), the <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> values for O1, O2, R1, and R2 are estimated to be 1.28 × 10<sup>–10</sup>, 1.44 × 10<sup>–10</sup>, 9.32 × 10<sup>–11</sup>, and 7.19 × 10<sup>–11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. The further investigation of the surface- and diffusion-limited processes was conducted through the equation of <i>i</i> = <i>av</i><sup><i>b</i></sup>(<i>lni</i> = <i>blnv</i> + <i>lna</i>), in which <i>i</i> and <i>v</i> stand for the peak current and scan rate. Meanwhile, <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are both invariants, and <i>b</i> is between 0.5 for the diffusion-limited process and 1 for the surface-limited process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a> <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c displays the linear relationships of ln<i>i</i> versus ln<i>v</i>, and the <i>b</i> values of O1, O2, R1, and R2 are 0.7486, 0.6867, 0.7453, and 0.5535, implying a combination effect of the diffusion-limited intercalation and surface-limited capacitive behaviors for Na storage. The pseudocapacitive contribution is quantified by the formula of <i>i</i> = <i>k</i><sub>1</sub>v + <i>k</i><sub>2</sub>v<sup>1/2</sup>, in which <i>k</i><sub>1</sub>v and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub>v<sup>1/2</sup> are related to the capacitive and diffusion-limited currents. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref56" aria-label="reference 56">(56)</a> At the peak potential of O1, the calculated capacitive contributions are displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d. As the scan rate enlarges, the contribution ratio through the surface-limited reaction gradually strengthens. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>e presents the in situ XRD results of the initial two cycles. Peaks such as (113), (116), (300), and (226) gradually shift to the right during Na<sup>+</sup> extraction and return to its initial location in the process of Na<sup>+</sup> reinsertion. The corresponding contour map at the selected 2θ range with the lattice parameter and volume changes calculated by GSAS software are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>f. No asymmetric variations are observed, demonstrating no crystal distortion and an ideal electrochemical reversibility of the NVMP/C electrode during repeated desodiation and sodiation processes.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c14093/asset/images/medium/am4c14093_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c14093/asset/images/large/am4c14093_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Kinetics and Na storage investigations of NVMP/C electrodes. (a) CV profiles at various scan rates, (b) linear relationship for peak current and square root of scan rate, (c) <i>b</i>-value of redox peaks, (d) pseudocapacitive contribution at various scan rates, (e) in situ XRD patterns of the initial two cycles at 0.3 C, and (f) corresponding 2D contour map at the selected 2θ range with the lattice parameter and volume changes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c14093/asset/images/large/am4c14093_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c14093&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To demonstrate the electrochemical performance of the NVMP/C nanorods electrode in the full cells, the NVMP/C cathode, coupled with the commercial HC anode, resulted in the further fabrication of a coin-type full-cell battery. The electrochemical property of the HC anode is evaluated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a and displays a capacity of 232.2 mA h·g<sup>–1</sup> at 23 mA·g<sup>–1</sup>. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>b exhibits the first three charge–discharge curves (2.4–3.8 V), which exhibits a capacity of 87.9 mA h·g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2 C. After continuous cycling at 0.5 C for 100 cycles, the capacity retention is only 82.3% (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>c). Commonly known, the electrochemical properties of a full battery are profoundly influenced due to a series of factors, containing the properties of all key materials, configuration type chosen (coin, pouch, prismatic, or cylindrical), N/P ratio (negative capacity versus positive capacity), and so on. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57 ref58 ref59 ref60" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref57 ref58 ref59 ref60" aria-label="reference 57 to 60">(57−60)</a> Although there are still many obstacles, NVMP/C is an encouraging cathode for SIBs under certain condition.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c14093/asset/images/medium/am4c14093_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c14093/asset/images/large/am4c14093_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Electrochemical performances of full cells. (a) Initial charge–discharge curves of the HC anode, and (b) typical charge–discharge curves of the NVMP/C-HC full cell with (c) corresponding cycling performance.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c14093/asset/images/large/am4c14093_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c14093&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, NVMP/C porous nanorods were fabricated through electrospinning. In situ XRD results demonstrated their outstanding structural stability and crystal reversibility during cycling. Based on this, the obtained NVMP/C porous nanorods displayed a good rate performance, which was better than that of NVMP. After 800 cycles at 1 C, NVMP/C can also achieve a good cycling stability with ideal capacity retentions of 90.4%. Consequently, the current work promoted the development of NVMP in the nanocomposites.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.4c14093?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.4c14093</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Digital photos of the precursor and NVMP/C; refinement data of NVMP/C; carbon and sulfur results of NVMP/C; BET results of NVMP/C; SEM and TEM images of NVMP; high-temperature cycling performance of NVMP/C; and performance comparison between NVMP/C and other reported cathodes (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c14093/suppl_file/am4c14093_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c14093/suppl_file/am4c14093_si_001.pdf">am4c14093_si_001.pdf (545.57 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.4c14093" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

Chem.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>7</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">19</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">29</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1038/nchem.2085</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D7%26publication_year%3D2015%26pages%3D19-29%26journal%3DNat.%2BChem.%26author%3DD.%2BLarcher%26author%3DJ.-M.%2BTarascon%26title%3DTowards%2BGreener%2Band%2BMore%2BSustainable%2BBatteries%2Bfor%2BElectrical%2BEnergy%2BStorage%26doi%3D10.1038%252Fnchem.2085&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.4c14093&amp;doiOfLink=10.1038%2Fnchem.2085&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Towards greener and more sustainable batteries for electrical energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Larcher, D.; Tarascon, J.-M.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Chemistry</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">19-29</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NCAHBB</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1755-4330</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Ever-growing energy needs and depleting fossil-fuel resources demand the pursuit of sustainable energy alternatives, including both renewable energy sources and sustainable storage technologies.  It is therefore essential to incorporate material abundance, eco-efficient synthetic processes and life-cycle anal. into the design of new electrochem. storage systems.  At present, a few existing technologies address these issues, but in each case, fundamental and technol. hurdles remain to be overcome.  Here the authors provide an overview of the current state of energy storage from a sustainability perspective.  The authors introduce the notion of sustainability through discussion of the energy and environmental costs of state-of-the-art Li-ion batteries, considering elemental abundance, toxicity, synthetic methods and scalability.  With the same themes in mind, the authors also highlight current and future electrochem. storage systems beyond Li-ion batteries. Room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries have attracted great attention particularly in large-scale elec. energy storage applications for renewable energy and smart grid because of the huge abundant sodium resources and low cost.  In this article, a variety of electrode materials including cathodes and anodes as well as electrolytes for room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries are briefly reviewed.  We compare the difference in storage behavior between Na and Li in their analogous electrodes and summarize the sodium storage mechanisms in the available electrode materials.  This review also includes some new results from our group and our thoughts on developing new materials.  Some perspectives and directions on designing better materials for practical applications are pointed out based on knowledge from the literature and our experience.  Through this extensive literature review, the search for suitable electrode and electrolyte materials for stationary sodium-ion batteries is still challenging. In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries have been considered promising candidates for stationary energy storage.  However, their wide application is hindered by issues such as high cost and insufficient electrochem. performance, particularly for cathode materials.  Here, we report a solvent-free mechanochem. protocol for the in-situ fabrication of sodium vanadium fluorophosphates.                         cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Polyanion-type cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jin Ting; Li Huangxu; Zhu Kunjie; Wang Peng-Fei; Liu Pei; Jiao Lifang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">49</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2342-2377</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn"></span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Room-temperature sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are regarded as promising candidates for smart grids and large-scale energy storage systems (EESs) due to their significant benefits of abundant and low-cost sodium resource.  Among the previously reported cathode materials for SIBs, layered transition-metal oxides and polyanion-type materials are considered to be the most attractive options.  Although many layered transition-metal oxides can provide high capacity due to their small molecular weight, their further application is hindered by low output voltage (mostly lower than 3.5 V), irreversible phase transition as well as storage instability.  Comparatively, polyanion-type materials exhibit higher operating potentials due to the inductive effect of polyanion groups.  Their robust 3D framework significantly decreases the structural variations during sodium ion de/intercalation.  Moreover, the effect of strong X-O (X = S, P, Si, etc.) covalent bonds can effectively inhibit oxygen evolution.  These advantages contribute to the superior cycle stability and high safety of polyanion-type materials.  However, low electronic conductivity and limited capacity still restrict their further application.  This review summarizes the recent progress of polyanion-type materials for SIBs, which include phosphates, fluorophosphates, pyrophosphates, mixed phosphates, sulfates, and silicates.  We also discuss the remaining challenges and corresponding strategies for polyanion-type materials.  We hope this review can provide some insights into the development of polyanionic materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref10" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1038/s41467-020-14444-4"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, Q.-F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chou, S.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.-K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dou, S.-X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Reversible Structural Evolution of Sodium-Rich Rhombohedral Prussian Blue for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Nat.                         cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Reversible structural evolution of sodium-rich rhombohedral Prussian blue for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Wanlin; Gang, Yong; Hu, Zhe; Yan, Zichao; Li, Weijie; Li, Yongcheng; Gu, Qin-Fen; Wang, Zhixing; Chou, Shu-Lei; Liu, Hua-Kun; Dou, Shi-Xue</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">980</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Research</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Iron-based Prussian blue analogs are promising low-cost and easily prepd. cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries.  Their materials quality and electrochem. performance are heavily reliant on the pptn. process.  Here we report a controllable pptn. method to synthesize high-performance Prussian blue for sodium-ion storage.  Characterization of the nucleation and evolution processes of the highly cryst. Prussian blue microcubes reveals a rhombohedral structure that exhibits high initial Coulombic efficiency, excellent rate performance, and cycling properties.  The phase transitions in the as-obtained material are investigated by synchrotron in situ powder X-ray diffraction, which shows highly reversible structural transformations between rhombohedral, cubic, and tetragonal structures upon sodium-ion (de)intercalations.  Moreover, the Prussian blue material from a large-scale synthesis process shows stable cycling performance in a pouch full cell over 1000 times.  We believe that this work could pave the way for the real application of Prussian blue materials in sodium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref11" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2017.07.005"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xie, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, R.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Novel Border-Rich Prussian Blue Synthetized by Inhibitor Control as Cathode for Sodium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Nano Energy</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>39</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">273</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">283</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A novel border-rich Prussian blue synthetized by inhibitor control as cathode for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Huang, Yongxin; Xie, Man; Zhang, Jiatao; Wang, Ziheng; Jiang, Ying; Xiao, Genhua; Li, Shuaijie; Li, Li; Wu, Feng; Chen, Renjie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The performance of a Prussian blue cathode is affected by its structure and stability.  Through the inhibitor and temp. control, the border-rich structure was obtained, which provides a good contact between electrode-electrolyte interfaces and increases the transmission path for Na+ ions.  In addn., the as-prepd. sample with rhombohedral phase demonstrated lower band gap and lower energy barrier for Na+ ions insertion.  Benefiting from the kinetics optimization, the as-prepd. electrode exhibited initial capacity of 120 mA h g-1 and maintained nearly 80% after 280 cycles at c.d. of 100 mA g-1.  This electrode also exhibited good rate performance about 60 mA h g-1 at rate of 10 C.  The structural stability is also related to the formation of the passivation layer during the charge-discharge process.  The optimized passivation layer not only protects the electrode form the adverse side reactions at high voltage but also delivers low interface impedance.  It can be inferred that the passivation layer plays a pos. role in long cycling performance.  When the as-prepd. electrode was measured in an electrolyte with FEC additive, it exhibited a high capacity retention rate of 79% after 500 cycles.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref12" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Deng, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Research Progress of Electroactive Organic Electrode Materials for Sodium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">High-energy batteries need significant cathodes which can simultaneously provide large specific capacities and high discharge plateaus.
